padding-right:0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Linguasphere_Observatory" title="Linguasphere Observatory">Linguasphere</a></th><td style="line-height:1.35em;">\n<code>59-AAF-h</code></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height:1.35em;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Idioma_guyarat%C3%AD.png" class="image"><img alt="Idioma guyarat\xc3\xad.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Idioma_guyarat%C3%AD.png/220px-Idioma_guyarat%C3%AD.png" width="220" height="219" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/31/Idioma_guyarat%C3%AD.png 1.5x" data-file-width="330" data-file-height="328" /></a><div style="text-align:left;">Distribution of native Gujarati speakers in India</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Gujarati</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x8c/: secondary stress follows">\xcb\x8c</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span><span title="/\xca\x8a/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;push&#39;">\xca\x8a</span><span title="/d\xca\x92/: &#39;j&#39; in &#39;jam&#39;">d\xca\x92</span><span title="/\xc9\x99/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">\xc9\x99</span><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/\xc9\x91\xcb\x90/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">\xc9\x91\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <span lang="gu" title="Gujarati language text">\xe0\xaa\x97\xe0\xab\x81\xe0\xaa\x9c\xe0\xaa\xb0\xe0\xaa\xbe\xe0\xaa\xa4\xe0\xab\x80</span> <i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i>gujar\xc4\x81t\xc4\xab</i></i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Gujarati" title="Help:IPA/Gujarati">[\xc9\xa1ud\xca\x92\xc9\x99\xcb\x88\xc9\xbe\xc9\x91\xcb\x90ti]</a></span>) is an <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan language</a> native to the <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> state of <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>. It is part of the greater <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language family</a>. Gujarati is descended from <a href="/wiki/Old_Gujarati" title="Old Gujarati">Old Gujarati</a> (<i>circa</i> 1100\xe2\x80\x931500&#160;AD). In India, it is the official language in the state of <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, as well as an official language in the <a href="/wiki/Union_territory" title="Union territory">union territories</a> of <a href="/wiki/Daman_and_Diu" title="Daman and Diu">Daman and Diu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dadra_and_Nagar_Haveli" title="Dadra and Nagar Haveli">Dadra and Nagar Haveli</a>. As of 2011, Gujarati is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers_in_India" title="List of languages by number of native speakers in India">6th most widely spoken language in India</a> by number of native speakers, spoken by 55.5 million speakers which amounts to about 4.5% of the total Indian population.<sup id="cite_ref-census2011_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census2011-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">26th most widely spoken language</a> in the world by number of native speakers as of 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-Nationalencyklopedin_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationalencyklopedin-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Gujarati language is more than 700 years old and is spoken by more than 55&#160;million people worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Outside of Gujarat, Gujarati is spoken in many other parts of South Asia by Gujarati migrants, especially in <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> (mainly in <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Gujarati is also widely spoken in many countries outside South Asia by the Gujarati diaspora. In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, Gujarati is one of the fastest growing and most widely-spoken Indian languages in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, Gujaratis form the second largest of the <a href="/wiki/British_Asian" title="British Asian">British South Asian</a> speech communities, and Gujarati is the fourth most commonly spoken language in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">the U.K.</a>\'s capital <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Gujarati is also spoken in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Africa" title="Southeast Africa">Southeast Africa</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-e20_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e20-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere, Gujarati is spoken to a lesser extent in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>), <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Eastern">Middle Eastern</a> countries such as <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-e20_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e20-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gujarati was the mother tongue of <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Jinnah" title="Muhammad Ali Jinnah">Muhammad Ali Jinnah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#References"><span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jinnah_Gandhi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Jinnah_Gandhi.jpg/250px-Jinnah_Gandhi.jpg" width="250" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Jinnah_Gandhi.jpg/375px-Jinnah_Gandhi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/Jinnah_Gandhi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="308" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jinnah_Gandhi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Jinnah" title="Muhammad Ali Jinnah">Muhammad Ali Jinnah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> sharing a <a href="/wiki/Laughter" title="Laughter">laugh</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bombay" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombay">Bombay</a> in 1944, for ill-fated political talks. These two prime political figures of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> in the 20th century were <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_people" title="Gujarati people">Gujaratis</a> and native speakers of the Gujarati language.<br /><br />For Jinnah, Gujarati was important only as mother tongue. He was neither born nor raised in <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-story_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-story-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and Gujarat did not end up a part of Pakistan, the state he espoused. He went on to advocate for solely <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Movement" title="Pakistan Movement">politics</a>.<br /><br />For Gandhi, Gujarati served as a medium of literary expression. He helped to inspire a renewal in its literature,<sup id="cite_ref-Dalby_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalby-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and in 1936 he introduced the current spelling convention at the <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_Sahitya_Parishad" title="Gujarati Sahitya Parishad">Gujarati Literary Society</a>\'s 12th meeting.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Upadesamala2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Upadesamala2.jpg/220px-Upadesamala2.jpg" width="220" height="104" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Upadesamala2.jpg/330px-Upadesamala2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Upadesamala2.jpg/440px-Upadesamala2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="984" data-file-height="465" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Upadesamala2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Updeshmala, Manuscript in Jain Prakrit and Old Gujarati on paper, Rupnagar, Rajastan, India, 1666, 76 ff. (\xe2\x88\x9216 ff.), 11x25 cm, single column, (10x22 cm), 4 lines main text, 2\xe2\x80\x934 lines of interlinear commentary for each text line, in Jain Devanagari book script, filled with red and yellow, 17 paintings in colours mostly of <a href="/wiki/Svetambara" class="mw-redirect" title="Svetambara">Svetambara</a> <a href="/wiki/Jain" class="mw-redirect" title="Jain">Jain</a> monks, influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_painting" title="Mughal painting">Mughal style</a>.<br /><br />The text is a Prakrit didactic work of how best to live a proper Jain life, aimed probably at the laity. The Svetambara pontiff, Sri Dharmadasaga\xc3\xae, lived in the mid-6th century. The Old Gujarati prose commentary was written in 1487. The colophon gives the place, date, and the name of the religious leader, Sri Namdalalaji, on whose order the work was transcribed.</div></div></div>\n<p>Gujarati (also sometimes spelled <i>Gujerati</i>, <i>Gujarathi</i>, <i>Guzratee</i>, <i>Guujaratee</i>, "Gujarati", <i>Gujrathi</i>, and <i>Gujerathi</i><sup id="cite_ref-e18_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e18-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mistry2001p274_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mistry2001p274-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>) is a modern IA (Indo-Aryan) language <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_linguistics" title="Evolutionary linguistics">evolved</a> from <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>. The traditional practice is to differentiate the IA languages on the basis of three historical stages:<sup id="cite_ref-Mistry2001p274_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mistry2001p274-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ol><li>Old IA (<a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">Vedic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Classical</a> Sanskrit)</li>\n<li>Middle IA (various <a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apabhramsha" class="mw-redirect" title="Apabhramsha">Apabhramshas</a>)</li>\n<li>New IA (modern languages such as <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Et_cetera" title="Et cetera">etc.</a>)</li></ol>\n<p>Another view postulates successive family tree splits, in which Gujarati is assumed to have separated from other IA languages in four stages:<sup id="cite_ref-M2_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ol><li>IA languages split into Northern, Eastern, and Western divisions based on the innovate characteristics such as <a href="/wiki/Plosive" class="mw-redirect" title="Plosive">plosives</a> becoming <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voiced</a> in the Northern (Skt. <i>danta</i> "<a href="/wiki/Tooth" title="Tooth">tooth</a>" &gt; Punj. <i>d\xc4\x81nd</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Dental_consonant" title="Dental consonant">dental</a> and <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">retroflex</a> <a href="/wiki/Sibilant" title="Sibilant">sibilants</a> merging with the <a href="/wiki/Palatal" class="mw-redirect" title="Palatal">palatal</a> in the Eastern (Skt. <i>sandhya</i>  "<a href="/wiki/Evening" title="Evening">evening</a>" &gt; Beng. <i>\xc5\x9b\xc4\x81jh</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Western, into Central and Southern.</li>\n<li>Central, in Gujarati/<a href="/wiki/Rajasthani_language" title="Rajasthani language">Rajasthani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Hindi" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Hindi">Western Hindi</a>, and Punjabi/Lahanda/Sindhi, on the basis of innovation of <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">auxiliary verbs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Postpositions" class="mw-redirect" title="Postpositions">postpositions</a> in Gujarati/Rajasthani.<sup id="cite_ref-Mistry2001p274_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mistry2001p274-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Gujarati/Rajasthani into Gujarati and Rajasthani through development of such characteristics as auxiliary <i>ch</i>- and the <a href="/wiki/Possession_(linguistics)" title="Possession (linguistics)">possessive</a> <a href="/wiki/Marker_(linguistics)" title="Marker (linguistics)">marker</a> -<i>n</i>- during the 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>\n<p>The principal changes from Sanskrit are the following:<sup id="cite_ref-M2_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M2-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">Phonological</a>\n<ul><li>Loss of original <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemic</a> <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">length</a> for <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowels</a></li>\n<li>Change of <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">consonant clusters</a> to <a href="/wiki/Consonant_length" class="mw-redirect" title="Consonant length">geminate</a> and then to single <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonants</a> (with compensatory vowel length)</li></ul></li></ul>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>English</th>\n<th>Sanskrit</th>\n<th><a id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/7" title="7">seven</a></td>\n<td><i>sapta</i></td>\n<td><i>satta</i></td>\n<td><i>s\xc4\x81t</i></td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/8" title="8">eight</a></td>\n<td><i>a\xe1\xb9\xa3\xe1\xb9\xad\xc4\x81</i></td>\n<td><i>a\xe1\xb9\xad\xe1\xb9\xadha</i></td>\n<td><i>\xc4\x81\xe1\xb9\xadh</i></td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Snake" href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">Morphological</a>\n<ul><li>Reduction in the number of <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compounds</a></li>\n<li>Merger of the <a href="/wiki/Dual_(grammatical_number)" title="Dual (grammatical number)">dual</a> with <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a></li>\n<li>Replacement of <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">case</a> <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affixes</a> by postpositions</li>\n<li>Development of <a href="/wiki/Periphrasis" title="Periphrasis">periphrastic</a> <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical constructions</li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">Syntax</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Split_ergativity" title="Split ergativity">Split ergativity</a></li>\n<li>More complex <a href="/wiki/Agreement_(linguistics)" title="Agreement (linguistics)">agreement</a> system</li></ul></li></ul>\n<p>Gujarati is then customarily divided into the following three historical stages:<sup id="cite_ref-Mistry2001p274_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mistry2001p274-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Gujarati">Old Gujarati</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Old Gujarati">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Gujarati_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Gujarati language">Old Gujarati language</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Gujarati">Middle Gujarati</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Middle Gujarati">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Middle_Gujarati_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Gujarati language">Middle Gujarati language</a></div>\n<h3><span id="Modern_Gujarati_.28AD_1800.E2.80.93_.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Gujarati_(AD_1800\xe2\x80\x93_)">Modern Gujarati (AD 1800\xe2\x80\x93 )</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern Gujarati (AD 1800\xe2\x80\x93 )">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Page_from_the_Gujarati_translation_of_%27Dabist%C4%81n-i_Maz%C4%81hibm%27_prepared_and_printed_by_Fardunji_Marzban_(1815).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/A_Page_from_the_Gujarati_translation_of_%27Dabist%C4%81n-i_Maz%C4%81hibm%27_prepared_and_printed_by_Fardunji_Marzban_%281815%29.jpg/220px-A_Page_from_the_Gujarati_translation_of_%27Dabist%C4%81n-i_Maz%C4%81hibm%27_prepared_and_printed_by_Fardunji_Marzban_%281815%29.jpg" href="/wiki/File:A_Page_from_the_Gujarati_translation_of_%27Dabist%C4%81n-i_Maz%C4%81hibm%27_prepared_and_printed_by_Fardunji_Marzban_(1815).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Page from the Gujarati translation of <i>Dabestan-e Mazaheb</i> prepared and printed by Fardunji Marzban (25 December 1815)</div></div></div>\n<p>A major phonological change was the deletion of final <i>\xc9\x99</i>, such that the modern language has consonant-final words. Grammatically, a new plural marker of -<i>o</i> developed.<sup id="cite_ref-CardonaSuthar661_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CardonaSuthar661-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In literature, the third quarter of the 19th century saw a series of milestones for Gujarati, which previously had had verse as its dominant mode of literary composition.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics_and_distribution">Demographics and distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Demographics and distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Administrative_map_of_Gujarat.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Administrative_map_of_Gujarat.png/280px-Administrative_map_of_Gujarat.png" width="280" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Administrative_map_of_Gujarat.png/420px-Administrative_map_of_Gujarat.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Administrative_map_of_Gujarat.png/560px-Administrative_map_of_Gujarat.png 2x" data-file-width="3014" data-file-height="2391" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Administrative_map_of_Gujarat.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Gujarat</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:India_Square_JC_jeh.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/India_Square_JC_jeh.JPG/220px-India_Square_JC_jeh.JPG" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/India_Square_JC_jeh.JPG/330px-India_Square_JC_jeh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/India_Square_JC_jeh.JPG/440px-India_Square_JC_jeh.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2042" data-file-height="1684" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:India_Square_JC_jeh.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/India_Square" title="India Square">India Square</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jersey_City,_New_Jersey" title="Jersey City, New Jersey">Jersey City</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, USA. Gujarati has achieved high linguistic prominence in many urban districts worldwide, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Metropolitan_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="New York City Metropolitan Area">New York City Metropolitan Area</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Of the approximately 46&#160;million speakers of Gujarati in 1997, roughly 45.5&#160;million resided in India, 150,000 in Uganda, 50,000 in <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, 50,000 in Kenya and roughly 100,000 in Karachi, Pakistan, excluding several hundreds of thousands of Memonis who do not self-identify as Gujarati, but hail from a region within the state of Gujarat.<sup id="cite_ref-e16_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e16-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>  However, <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_people" title="Gujarati people">Gujarati</a> community leaders in Pakistan claim that there are 3 million Gujarati speakers in Karachi.<sup id="cite_ref-:72_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:72-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere in Pakistan, Gujarati is also spoken in Lower <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Punjab, Pakistan">Punjab</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Frawley2003_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frawley2003-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Pakistani Gujarati is probably a dialect of Gamadia.<sup id="cite_ref-Frawley2003_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frawley2003-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is a certain amount of Mauritian population and a large amount of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union" title="R\xc3\xa9union">R\xc3\xa9union</a> Island people who are from Gujarati descent among which some of them still speak Gujarati.\n</p><p>A considerable Gujarati-speaking population exists in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, most particularly in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Metropolitan_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="New York City Metropolitan Area">New York City Metropolitan Area</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Toronto_Area" title="Greater Toronto Area">Greater Toronto Area</a>, which have over 100,000 speakers and over 75,000 speakers, respectively, but also throughout the major metropolitan areas of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. According to the 2011 census, Gujarati is the seventeenth most spoken language in the Greater Toronto Area, and the fourth most spoken South Asian language after <a href="/wiki/Hindi-Urdu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindi-Urdu">Hindi-Urdu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a>.\n</p><p>The UK has over 200,000 speakers, many of them situated in the <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> area, especially in North West London, but also in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Leicester" title="Leicester">Leicester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bradford" title="Bradford">Bradford</a> and the former mill towns within <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>. A portion of these numbers consists of <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East African</a> Gujaratis who, under increasing discrimination and policies of Africanisation in their newly independent resident countries (especially <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Idi_Amin" title="Idi Amin">Idi Amin</a> expelled 50,000 Asians), were left with uncertain futures and <a href="/wiki/Citizenship" title="Citizenship">citizenships</a>. Most, with British <a href="/wiki/Passport" title="Passport">passports</a>, settled in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-Dalby_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalby-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Gujarati is offered as a <a href="/wiki/GCSE" class="mw-redirect" title="GCSE">GCSE</a> subject for students in the UK.\n</p><p>Gujarati parents in the diaspora are not comfortable with the possibility of their language not surviving them.<sup id="cite_ref-lang_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lang-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In a study, 80% of <a href="/wiki/Malayali" title="Malayali">Malayali</a> parents felt that "Children would be better off with English", compared to 36% of <a href="/wiki/Kannada_people" title="Kannada people">Kannada</a> parents and only 19% of Gujarati parents.<sup id="cite_ref-lang_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lang-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Besides being spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_people" title="Gujarati people">Gujarati people</a>, non-Gujarati residents of and migrants to the state of <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a> also count as speakers, among them the <a href="/wiki/Kutch" class="mw-redirect" title="Kutch">Kutchis</a> (as a <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary language</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-Dalby_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalby-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Parsi_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Parsi people">Parsis</a> (adopted as a <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">mother tongue</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Sindhi people">Sindhi</a> <a href="/wiki/Refugees" class="mw-redirect" title="Refugees">refugees</a> from Pakistan.\nA distribution of the geographical area can be found in  \'<a href="/wiki/Linguistic_Survey_of_India" title="Linguistic Survey of India">Linguistic Survey of India</a>\' by <a href="/wiki/George_A._Grierson" class="mw-redirect" title="George A. Grierson">George A. Grierson</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Official status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Gujarati is one of the twenty-two <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official languages</a> and fourteen <a href="/wiki/Regional_language" title="Regional language">regional languages</a> of India. It is officially recognized in the state of Gujarat, India and the union territories of Dadra and Nagar Haveli and Daman and Diu.\n</p><p>Gujarati is recognized and taught as a minority language in the states of <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madhya_Pradesh" title="Madhya Pradesh">Madhya Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a> and the union territory of <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/States_of_India_by_Gujarati_speakers" class="mw-redirect" title="States of India by Gujarati speakers">States of India by Gujarati speakers</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>According to British historian and philologist <a href="/wiki/William_St._Clair_Tisdall" title="William St. Clair Tisdall">William Tisdall</a>, who was an early scholar of <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_grammar" title="Gujarati grammar">Gujarati grammar</a>, three major varieties of Gujarati exist: a standard \'Hindu\' dialect, a \'<a href="/wiki/Parsi" title="Parsi">Parsi</a>\' dialect and a \'<a href="/wiki/Muslim_Gujarati" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim Gujarati">Muslim</a>\' dialect.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, Gujarati has undergone contemporary reclassification with respect to the widespread regional differences in vocabulary and phrasing; notwithstanding the number of poorly attested dialects and regional variations in naming.\n</p>\n<ul><li>Standard Gujarati: this forms something of a standardised variant of Gujarati across news, education and government. It is also spoken in pockets of Maharashtra. The varieties of it include Mumbai Gujarati, Nagari, Patnuli, Saurashtra Standard.</li>\n<li>Gamadia: spoken primarily in Ahmedabad and the surrounding regions, in addition to Bharuch and Surat, where it is colloquially known as \'Surati\'. The varieties of it include Ahmedabad Gamadia, Anawla, Brathela, Charotari, Eastern Broach Gujarati, Gramya, Patani, Patidari, Surati, Vadodari.</li>\n<li>Kathiawari: a distinctive variant spoken primarily in the <a href="/wiki/Kathiawar" title="Kathiawar">Kathiawar</a> region and subject to significant Sindhi influence. The varieties of it include Bhavnagari, Gohilwadi, Holadi/Halari, Jhalawadi, Sorathi.</li></ul>\n<p>Kharwa, Kakari and Tarimuki (Ghisadi) are also often cited as additional varieties of Gujarati.\n</p>\n<ul><li>Parsi: spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Parsi" title="Parsi">Parsi</a> minority. This highly distinctive variety has been subject to considerable lexical influence by <a href="/wiki/Avestan" title="Avestan">Avestan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgical</a> Zoroastrian language.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lisan_ud-Dawat" title="Lisan ud-Dawat">Lisan ud-Dawat</a>: spoken primarily by <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Gujarati Muslim">Gujarati Muslim</a> <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Bohra" title="Sunni Bohra">Bohra</a> communities, it has been subject to greater lexical influence by <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and is written in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a>.</li></ul>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Kutchi_language" title="Kutchi language">Kutchi</a> is often referred to as a dialect of Gujarati, but most linguists consider it closer to <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">Sindhi</a>. In addition, a mixture between Sindhi, Gujarati, and Kutchi called <a href="/wiki/Memoni_language" title="Memoni language">Memoni</a> is related to Gujarati, albeit distantly.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Furthermore, words used by the native languages of areas where the <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_people" title="Gujarati people">Gujarati people</a> have become a diaspora community, such as <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> (<a href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" class="mw-redirect" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili</a>), have become loanwords in local dialects of Gujarati.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_languages" title="Gujarati languages">Gujarati languages</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_phonology" title="Gujarati phonology">Gujarati phonology</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant#Palatal" title="Palatal approximant">j</a></span>\n</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Flap_consonant" title="Flap consonant">Flap</a>\n</th>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Dental_and_alveolar_flaps" title="Dental and alveolar flaps">\xc9\xbe</a></span>\n</td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">Writing system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Writing system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_alphabet" title="Gujarati alphabet">Gujarati alphabet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_braille" class="mw-redirect" title="Gujarati braille">Gujarati braille</a></div>\n<p>Similar to other <a href="/wiki/Nagari" class="mw-redirect" title="Nagari">N\xc4\x81gar\xc4\xab</a> writing systems, the Gujarati script is an <a href="/wiki/Abugida" title="Abugida">abugida</a>. It is used to write the Gujarati and <a href="/wiki/Kutchi_language" title="Kutchi language">Kutchi</a> languages. It is a variant of <a href="/wiki/Devan%C4%81gar%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Devan\xc4\x81gar\xc4\xab">Devan\xc4\x81gar\xc4\xab</a> script differentiated by the loss of the characteristic horizontal line running above the letters and by a small number of modifications in the remaining characters.\n</p><p>Gujarati and closely related languages, including <a href="/wiki/Kutchi_language" title="Kutchi language">Kutchi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parkari_Koli_language" title="Parkari Koli language">Parkari Koli</a>, can be written in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Persian_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian script">Persian scripts</a>. This is traditionally done by many in Gujarat\'s <a href="/wiki/Kutch" class="mw-redirect" title="Kutch">Kutch</a> district.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Categorisation_and_sources">Categorisation and sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Categorisation and sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>These are the three general categories of words in modern Indo-Aryan: <i>tatsam</i>, <i>tadbhav</i>, and loanwords.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tadbhav">Tadbhav</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tadbhav">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><span lang="gu" title="Gujarati language text">\xe0\xaa\xa4\xe0\xaa\xa6\xe0\xab\x8d\xe0\xaa\xad\xe0\xaa\xb5</span> <i>tadbhava</i>, "of the nature of that". Gujarati is a modern Indo-Aryan language descended from <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> (old Indo-Aryan), and this category pertains exactly to that: words of Sanskritic origin that have demonstratively undergone change over the ages, ending up characteristic of modern Indo-Aryan languages specifically as well as in general. Thus the "that" in "of the nature of that" refers to Sanskrit. They tend to be non-technical, everyday, crucial words; part of the spoken vernacular. Below is a table of a few Gujarati <i>tadbhav</i> words and their Old Indo-Aryan sources:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2">Old Indo-Aryan\n</th>\n<th colspan="2">Gujarati\n</th>\n<th>Ref\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/I" class="extiw" title="wikt:I">I</a></td>\n<td><i>aham</i></td>\n<td><i>h\xc5\xa9</i></td>\n<td></td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>falls, slips</td>\n<td><i>khasati</i></td>\n<td><i>khasv\xc5\xa9</i></td>\n<td>to move</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>causes to move</td>\n<td><i>arpayati</i></td>\n<td><i>\xc4\x81pv\xc5\xa9</i></td>\n<td>to give</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/school" class="extiw" title="wikt:school">school</a></td>\n<td><i>naya\xc5\x9b\xc4\x81l\xc4\x81</i></td>\n<td><i>ni\xc5\x9b\xc4\x81\xe1\xb8\xb7</i></td>\n<td></td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>attains to, obtains</td>\n<td><i>pr\xc4\x81pnoti</i></td>\n<td><i>p\xc4\x81mv\xc5\xa9</i></td>\n<td></td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-48" title="wikt:tiger">tiger</a></td>\n<td><i>vy\xc4\x81ghra</i></td>\n<td><i>v\xc4\x81gh</i></td>\n<td></td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>equal, alike, level</td>\n<td><i>sama</i></td>\n<td><i>sam\xc5\xa9</i></td>\n<td>right, sound</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>all</td>\n<td><i>sarva</i></td>\n<td><i>sau</i></td>\n<td></td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tatsam">Tatsam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Tatsam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><span lang="gu" title="Gujarati language text">\xe0\xaa\xa4\xe0\xaa\xa4\xe0\xab\x8d\xe0\xaa\xb8\xe0\xaa\xae</span> <i>tatsama</i>, "same as that". While Sanskrit eventually stopped being spoken vernacularly, in that it changed into <a href="/wiki/Middle_Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Middle Indo-Aryan languages">Middle Indo-Aryan</a>, it was nonetheless standardized and retained as a literary and <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical language">liturgical language</a> for long after. This category consists of these borrowed words of (more or less) pure Sanskrit character. They serve to enrich Gujarati and modern Indo-Aryan in its formal, technical, and religious vocabulary. They are recognizable by their Sanskrit inflections and markings; they are thus often treated as a separate grammatical category unto themselves.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Tatsam\n</th>\n<th>English\n</th>\n<th>Gujarati\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i>lekhak</i></td>\n<td><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/writer" class="extiw" title="wikt:writer">writer</a></td>\n<td><i>lakhn\xc4\x81r</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i>vijet\xc4\x81</i></td>\n<td><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/winner" class="extiw" title="wikt:winner">winner</a></td>\n<td><i>j\xc4\xabtn\xc4\x81r</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i>vik\xc7\x8esit</i></td>\n<td><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/developed" class="extiw" title="wikt:developed">developed</a></td>\n<td><i>vik\xc7\x8esel\xc5\xa9</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i>j\xc4\x81g\xc7\x8era\xe1\xb9\x87</i></td>\n<td><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/awakening" class="extiw" title="wikt:awakening">awakening</a></td>\n<td><i>j\xc4\x81gv\xc4\x81n\xc5\xa9</i>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Many old tatsam words have changed their meanings or have had their meanings adopted for modern times. \xe0\xaa\xaa\xe0\xab\x8d\xe0\xaa\xb0\xe0\xaa\xb8\xe0\xaa\xbe\xe0\xaa\xb0\xe0\xaa\xa3 <i>pras\xc4\x81ra\xe1\xb9\x87</i> means "spreading", but now it is used for "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/broadcasting" class="extiw" title="wikt:broadcasting">broadcasting</a>". In addition to this are <a href="/wiki/Neologism" title="Neologism">neologisms</a>, often being <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calques</a>. An example is <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/telephone" class="extiw" title="wikt:telephone">telephone</a></i>, which is <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> for "far talk", translated as \xe0\xaa\xa6\xe0\xab\x81\xe0\xaa\xb0\xe0\xaa\xad\xe0\xaa\xbe\xe0\xaa\xb7 <i>durbh\xc4\x81\xe1\xb9\xa3</i>. Though most people just use \xe0\xaa\xab\xe0\xab\x8b\xe0\xaa\xa8 <i>phon</i> and thus neo-Sanskrit has varying degrees of acceptance.\n</p><p>So, while having unique <i>tadbhav</i> sets, modern IA languages have a common, higher <i>tatsam</i> pool. Also, <i>tatsam</i>s and their derived <i>tadbhav</i>s can also co-exist in a language; sometimes of no consequence and at other times with differences in meaning:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2">Tatsam\n</th>\n<th colspan="2">Tadbhav\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a></i></td>\n<td>Work \xe2\x80\x94 <a href="/wiki/Dharmic" class="mw-redirect" title="Dharmic">Dharmic</a> religious concept of works or deeds whose divine consequences are experienced in this life or the next.</td>\n<td><i>k\xc4\x81m</i></td>\n<td>work [without any religious connotations].\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i>k\xe1\xb9\xa3etra</i></td>\n<td>Field \xe2\x80\x94 Abstract sense, such as a field of knowledge or activity; <i>kh\xc4\x81ng\xc4\xab k\xe1\xb9\xa3etra</i> \xe2\x86\x92 private sector. Physical sense, but of higher or special importance; <i>ra\xe1\xb9\x87\xc7\x8ek\xe1\xb9\xa3etra</i> \xe2\x86\x92 battlefield.</td>\n<td><i>khetar</i></td>\n<td>field [in agricultural sense].\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>What remains are words of foreign origin (<i>vide\xc5\x9b\xc4\xab</i>), as well as words of local origin that cannot be pegged as belonging to any of the three prior categories (<i>de\xc5\x9baj</i>). The former consists mainly of <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, and English, with trace elements of <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkish</a>. While the phenomenon of English <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a> is relatively new, Perso-Arabic has a longer history behind it. Both English and Perso-Arabic influences are quite nationwide phenomena, in a way paralleling <i>tatsam</i> as a common vocabulary set or bank. What\'s more is how, beyond a transposition into general Indo-Aryan, the Perso-Arabic set has also been assimilated in a manner characteristic and relevant to the specific Indo-Aryan language it is being used in, bringing to mind <i>tadbhav</i>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Perso-Arabic">Perso-Arabic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Perso-Arabic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>India was ruled for many a century by Persian-speaking <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquest in the Indian subcontinent">Muslims</a>, amongst the most notable being the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turko</a>-<a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghan</a> <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Turco-Mongol" class="mw-redirect" title="Turco-Mongol">Turco-Mongol</a> <a href="/wiki/Mughal_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal dynasty">Mughal dynasty</a>. As a consequence Indian languages were changed greatly, with the large scale entry of Persian and its many Arabic loans into the Gujarati lexicon. One fundamental adoption was Persian\'s conjunction "that", <i>ke</i>. Also, while <i>tatsam</i> or Sanskrit is etymologically continuous to Gujarati, it is essentially of a differing grammar (or language), and that in comparison while Perso-Arabic is etymologically foreign, it has been in certain instances and to varying degrees grammatically indigenized. Owing to centuries of situation and the end of Persian education and power, (1) Perso-Arabic loans are quite unlikely to be thought of or known as loans, and (2) more importantly, these loans have often been Gujarati-ized. <i>d\xc4\x81vo</i> \xe2\x80\x93 claim, <i>f\xc4\x81ydo</i> \xe2\x80\x93 benefit, <i>nat\xc4\xabjo</i> \xe2\x80\x93 result, and <i>hamlo</i> \xe2\x80\x93 attack, all carry Gujarati\'s masculine gender marker, <i>o</i>. <i>kh\xc4\x81n\xc5\xa9</i> \xe2\x80\x93 compartment, has the neuter <i>\xc5\xa9</i>. Aside from easy slotting with the auxiliary <i>karv\xc5\xa9</i>, a few words have made a complete transition of verbification: <i>kab\xc5\xablv\xc5\xa9</i> \xe2\x80\x93 to admit (fault), <i>khar\xc4\xabdv\xc5\xa9</i> \xe2\x80\x93 to buy, <i>khar\xc7\x8ecv\xc5\xa9</i> \xe2\x80\x93 to spend (money), <i>gujarv\xc5\xa9</i> \xe2\x80\x93 to pass. The last three are definite part and parcel.\n</p><p>Below is a table displaying a number of these loans. Currently some of the etymologies are being referenced to an Urdu <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">dictionary</a> so that Gujarati\'s singular masculine <i>o</i> corresponds to Urdu <i>\xc4\x81</i>, neuter <i>\xc5\xa9</i> groups into <i>\xc4\x81</i> as Urdu has no neuter gender, and Urdu\'s Persian <i>z</i> is not upheld in Gujarati and corresponds to <i>j</i> or <i>jh</i>. In contrast to modern Persian, the pronunciation of these loans into Gujarati and other Indo-Aryan languages, as well as that of Indian-recited Persian, seems to be in line with Persian spoken in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, perhaps 500&#160;years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Masica75_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masica75-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="12">Nouns\n</th>\n<th rowspan="2" title="feminine">f</abbr>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i>f\xc4\x81ydo</i></td>\n<td>gain, advantage, benefit</td>\n<td>A</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a cold</a></td>\n<td>P</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td><i>jud\xc5\xa9</i></td>\n<td>different, separate</td>\n<td>P</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td><i>nas\xc4\xabb</i></td>\n<td><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/luck" class="extiw" title="wikt:luck">luck</a></td>\n<td>A</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td><i>b\xc4\x81ju</i></td>\n<td>side</td>\n<td>P</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td><i>naj\xc4\xabk</i></td>\n<td>near</td>\n<td>P</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i>nat\xc4\xabjo</i></td>\n<td>result, outcome</td>\n<td>A</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td><i>\xc5\x9baher</i></td>\n<td><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/city" class="extiw" title="wikt:city">city</a></td>\n<td>P</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Lastly, Persian, being part of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian language family</a> as Sanskrit and Gujarati are, met up in some instances with its cognates:<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Persian</th>\n<th>Indo-Aryan</th>\n<th>English\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i>mar\xc4\x83d</i></td>\n<td><i>martya</i></td>\n<td>man, mortal\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/-stan" title="-stan">st\xc4\x81n</a></i></td>\n<td><i>sth\xc4\x81n</i></td>\n<td>place, fastened\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a> Persian <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugees</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Parsi_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Parsi people">Parsis</a> also speak an accordingly Persianized form of Gujarati.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="English">English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fruit_juice_shop_in_Navsari.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Fruit_juice_shop_in_Navsari.jpg/280px-Fruit_juice_shop_in_Navsari.jpg" width="280" height="105" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Fruit_juice_shop_in_Navsari.jpg/420px-Fruit_juice_shop_in_Navsari.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Fruit_juice_shop_in_Navsari.jpg/560px-Fruit_juice_shop_in_Navsari.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1878" data-file-height="702" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fruit_juice_shop_in_Navsari.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>\xc5\x9br\xc4\xab sarasvat\xc4\xab fru\xe1\xb9\xad jyu\xc5\x9b s\xc9\x9bn\xe1\xb9\xadar</i> \xe2\x80\x93 "<a href="/wiki/Sri" title="Sri">Shri</a> <a href="/wiki/Saraswati" title="Saraswati">Saraswati</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fruit" class="extiw" title="wikt:fruit">fruit</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/juice" class="extiw" title="wikt:juice">juice</a> Centre". Note that "Fruit Juice Centre" is in English. A Gujarati alternative would be <i>pha\xe1\xb8\xb7n\xc4\x81 ras nu kendra</i>. It (<i>kendra</i> in particular) would however sound quite <a href="/wiki/Pedantic" class="mw-redirect" title="Pedantic">pedantic</a> and out of place.</div></div></div>\n<p>With the end of Perso-Arabic inflow, English became the current foreign source of new vocabulary. English had and continues to have a considerable influence over Indian languages. Loanwords include new innovations and concepts, first introduced directly through <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British colonialism</a>, and then streaming in on the basis of continued <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> dominance in the post-colonial period. Besides the category of new ideas is the category of English words that already have Gujarati counterparts which end up replaced or existed alongside with. The major driving force behind this latter category has to be the continuing role of English in modern India as a language of education, prestige, and mobility. In this way, Indian speech can be sprinkled with English words and expressions, even switches to whole sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> <i>See <a href="/wiki/Hinglish" title="Hinglish">Hinglish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Code-switching" title="Code-switching">Code-switching</a></i>.\n</p><p>In matters of sound, English <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_consonant" title="Alveolar consonant">alveolar</a> consonants map as <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">retroflexes</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Dental_consonant" title="Dental consonant">dentals</a>. Two new characters were created in Gujarati to represent English /\xc3\xa6/\'s and /\xc9\x94/\'s. Levels of Gujarati-ization in sound vary. Some words don\'t go far beyond this basic transpositional rule, and sound much like their English source, while others differ in ways, one of those ways being the carrying of dentals. <i>See <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">Indian English</a></i>.\n</p><p>As English loanwards are a relatively new phenomenon, they adhere to English grammar, as <i>tatsam</i> words adhere to Sanskrit. Though that isn\'t to say that the most basic changes have been underway: many English words are pluralized with Gujarati <i>o</i> over English "s". Also, with Gujarati having three genders, genderless English words must take one. Though often inexplicable, gender assignment may follow the same basis as it is expressed in Gujarati: vowel type, and the nature of word meaning.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" aunts but are of the age.</li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese">Portuguese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Portuguese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The smaller foothold the Portuguese had in wider India had linguistic effects. Gujarati took up a number of words, while elsewhere the influence was great enough to the extent that creole languages came to be (<i>see <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_India" title="Portuguese India">Portuguese India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese-based_creole_languages#India_and_Sri_Lanka" title="Portuguese-based creole languages">Portuguese-based creole languages in India and Sri Lanka</a></i>). Comparatively, the impact of Portuguese has been greater on coastal languages<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and their loans tend to be closer to the Portuguese originals.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The source dialect of these loans imparts an earlier pronunciation of <i>ch</i> as an affricate instead of the current standard of [\xca\x83].<sup id="cite_ref-Masica75_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masica75-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" title="Surname">surname</a>.</dd>\n<dd><sup>3</sup> "Master".</dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loans_into_English">Loans into English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Loans into English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Bungalow" title="Bungalow">Bungalow</a></i>\xe2\x80\x94\n</p>\n<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9c\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">1676, from Gujarati <i>bangalo</i>, from Hindi <i>bangla</i> "low, thatched house," lit. "Bengalese," used elliptically for "house in the Bengal style."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9d\n</td></tr>\n\n</tbody></table>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Coolie" title="Coolie">Coolie</a></i>\xe2\x80\x94\n</p>\n<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9c\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">1598, "name given by Europeans to hired laborers in India and China," from Hindi <i>quli</i> "hired servant," probably from <i>koli</i>, name of an aboriginal tribe or caste in Gujarat.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9d\n</td></tr>\n\n</tbody></table>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Tank_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Tank (disambiguation)">Tank</a></i>\xe2\x80\x94\n</p>\n<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9c\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">c.1616, "pool or lake for irrigation or drinking water," a word originally brought by the Portuguese from India, ult. from Gujarati <i>tankh</i> "cistern, underground reservoir for water," Marathi <i>tanken</i>, or <i>tanka</i> "reservoir of water, tank." Perhaps from Skt. <i>tadaga-m</i> "pond, lake pool," and reinforced in later sense of "large artificial container for liquid" (1690) by Port. <i>tanque</i> "reservoir," from <i>estancar</i> "hold back a current of water," from V.L. *<i>stanticare</i> (see <i>stanch</i>). But others say the Port. word is the source of the Indian ones.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9d\n</td></tr>\n\n</tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gujarati_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_grammar" title="Gujarati grammar">Gujarati grammar</a></div>\n<p>Gujarati is a head-final, or left-<a href="/wiki/Branching_(linguistics)" title="Branching (linguistics)">branching</a> language. <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">Adjectives</a> precede <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Direct_object" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct object">direct objects</a> come before <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verbs</a>, and there are <a href="/wiki/Postposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Postposition">postpositions</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a> of Gujarati is <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" title="Subject\xe2\x80\x93object\xe2\x80\x93verb">SOV</a>, and there are three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">genders</a> and two <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">numbers</a>. There are no <a href="/wiki/Definite_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Definite article">definite</a> or <a href="/wiki/Indefinite_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Indefinite article">indefinite articles</a>. A verb is expressed with its <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">verbal root</a> followed by <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffixes</a> marking <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agreement_(linguistics)" title="Agreement (linguistics)">agreement</a> in what is called a main form, with a possible proceeding <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">auxiliary</a> form derived from <i>to be</i>, marking <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tense</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">mood</a>, and also showing agreement. <a href="/wiki/Causative" title="Causative">Causatives</a> (up to double) and passives have a morphological basis.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_text">Sample text</span><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gandhi%27s_hut-karadi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" \xe0\xa4\xa4\xe0\xa5\x87\xe0\xa4\xae\xe0\xa4\xa3\xe0\xa5\x87 \xe0\xa4\xa7\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xb8\xe0\xa4\xa3\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa4\xbe \xe0\xa4\xae\xe0\xa5\x80\xe0\xa4\xa0\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa4\xbe \xe0\xa4\x85\xe0\xa4\x97\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa5\x8b \xe0\xa4\xa4\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa4\xab \xe0\xa4\x95\xe0\xa5\x82\xe0\xa4\x9a href="/wiki/Translation" title="Translation">Translation</a> \xe2\x80\x94</dt></dl>\n<dl><dd>Gandhiji\'s hut-Karadi</dd></dl>\n<dl><dd>After the world-famous <a href="/wiki/Salt_Satyagraha" class="mw-redirect" title="Salt Satyagraha">Dandi March</a> Gandhiji resided here in a <a href="/wiki/Date_palm" title="Date palm">date palm</a> <a href="/wiki/Bark_(botany)" title="Bark (botany)">bark</a> hut underneath a/the <a href="/wiki/Mango_tree" class="mw-redirect" title="Mango tree">mango tree</a>, from 14-4-1930 to 4-5-1930. From here he gave speed to and spread country-wide the anti-Salt Law struggle, started in Dandi on 6 April. From <i>here</i>, writing in a letter, he notified the British Viceroy of his resolve of marching towards the <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> <a href="/wiki/Mound" title="Mound">mounds</a> of Dharasana.</dd></dl>\n<dl><dd>The British government arrested him at this location, after twelve o\'clock on the night of 4 May 1930.</dd></dl>\n<p><b>Translation</b> (provided at location) \xe2\x80\x94\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Gandhiji\'s hut-Karadi</dd></dl>\n<dl><dd>Here under the mango tree in the hut made of palm leaves (khajoori) Gandhiji stayed from 14-4-1930 to 4-5-1930 after the world famous Dandi march. From here he gave impetus to the civil disobedience movement for breaking the salt act started on 6 April at Dandi and turned it into a nationwide movement. It was also from this place that he wrote a letter to the British viceroy expressing his firm resolve to march to the salt works at Dharasana.</dd></dl>\n<dl><dd>This is the place from where he was arrested by the British government after midnight on 4 May 1930.</dd></dl>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
